<p><b>Art Spiegelman</b> (born <b>Itzhak Avraham ben Zeev</b> on February 15, 1948) is an American cartoonist, editor, and comics advocate best known for his <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">graphic novel</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Maus" title="Maus">Maus</a></i>. His work as co-editor on the comics magazines <i><a href="/wiki/Arcade_(comics_magazine)" title="Arcade (comics magazine)">Arcade</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Raw_(magazine)" title="Raw (magazine)">Raw</a></i> has been influential, and from 1992 he spent a decade as contributing artist for <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>, where he made several high-profile and sometimes controversial covers. He is married to designer and editor <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly" title="FranÃ§oise Mouly">FranÃ§oise Mouly</a> and is the father of writer <a href="/wiki/Nadja_Spiegelman" title="Nadja Spiegelman">Nadja Spiegelman</a>.</p>
<p>Spiegelman began his career with the <a href="/wiki/Topps" title="Topps">Topps</a> bubblegum card company in the mid-1960s, which was his main financial support for two decades; there he co-created parodic series such as <i><a href="/wiki/Wacky_Packages" title="Wacky Packages">Wacky Packages</a></i> in the 1960s and the <i><a href="/wiki/Garbage_Pail_Kids" title="Garbage Pail Kids">Garbage Pail Kids</a></i> in the 1980s. He gained prominence in the <a href="/wiki/Underground_comix" title="Underground comix">underground comix</a> scene in the 1970s with short, experimental, and often autobiographical work. A selection of these strips appeared in the collection <i><a href="/wiki/Breakdowns_(comics)" title="Breakdowns (comics)">Breakdowns</a></i> in 1977, after which Spiegelman turned focus to the book-length <i>Maus</i>, about his relation with his father, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a> survivor. The postmodern book depicts Nazis as cats, Jews as mice, and ethnic Poles as pigs, and took thirteen years until its completion in 1991. It won a special <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a> in 1992 and has gained a reputation as a pivotal work, responsible for bringing scholarly attention to the comics medium.</p>
<p>Spiegelman and Mouly edited eleven issues of <i>Raw</i> from 1980 to 1991. The oversized comics and graphics magazine helped introduce talents who became prominent in <a href="/wiki/Alternative_comics" title="Alternative comics">alternative comics</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Burns_(cartoonist)" title="Charles Burns (cartoonist)">Charles Burns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Ware" title="Chris Ware">Chris Ware</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ben_Katchor" title="Ben Katchor">Ben Katchor</a>, and introduced several foreign cartoonists to the English-speaking comics world. Beginning in the 1990s, the couple worked for <i>The New Yorker</i>, which Spiegelman left to work on <i><a href="/wiki/In_the_Shadow_of_No_Towers" title="In the Shadow of No Towers">In the Shadow of No Towers</a></i> (2004), about his reaction to the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in New York in 2001.</p>
<p>Spiegelman advocates for greater comics literacy. As an editor, a teacher at the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Visual_Arts" title="School of Visual Arts">School of Visual Arts</a> in New York City, and a lecturer, Spiegelman has promoted better understanding of comics and has mentored younger cartoonists.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_history">Family history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Family history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sosnowiec_Ghetto_liquidation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="941" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Sosnowiec_Ghetto_liquidation.jpg/220px-Sosnowiec_Ghetto_liquidation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Sosnowiec_Ghetto_liquidation.jpg/330px-Sosnowiec_Ghetto_liquidation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Sosnowiec_Ghetto_liquidation.jpg/440px-Sosnowiec_Ghetto_liquidation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sosnowiec_Ghetto_liquidation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Liquidation at the <a href="/wiki/Sosnowiec_Ghetto" title="Sosnowiec Ghetto">Sosnowiec Ghetto</a> in <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Poland_(1939%E2%80%9345)" title="Occupation of Poland (1939â45)">occupied Poland</a> during World WarÂ II; Spiegelman tells of his parents' survival in <i><a href="/wiki/Maus" title="Maus">Maus</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Art Spiegelman's parents were Polish <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> WÅadysÅaw (1906â1982) and Andzia (1912â1968) Spiegelman. His father was born Zeev Spiegelman, with the Hebrew name Zeev ben Avraham. WÅadysÅaw was his Polish name, and WÅadek (or Vladek in <a href="/wiki/Russification" title="Russification">Russified</a> form) was a diminutive of this name. He was also known as Wilhelm under <a href="/wiki/History_of_Poland_(1939%E2%80%9345)" title="History of Poland (1939â45)">the German occupation</a>, and upon immigration to the United States he took the name William. His mother was born Andzia Zylberberg, with the Hebrew name Hannah. She took the name Anna upon her immigration to the US. In Spiegelman's <i><a href="/wiki/Maus" title="Maus">Maus</a></i>, from which the couple are best known, Spiegelman used the spellings "Vladek" and "Anja", which he believed would be easier for Americans to pronounce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpiegelman201116_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpiegelman201116-3">[3]</a></sup> The surname <i>Spiegelman</i> is German for "mirror man".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeicholz2008_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeicholz2008-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Spiegelmans had one other son, Rysio (spelled "Richieu" in <i>Maus</i>), who died before Art was born<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpiegelman201118_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpiegelman201118-1">[1]</a></sup> at the age of five or six.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHatfield2005146_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHatfield2005146-5">[5]</a></sup> During the Holocaust, Spiegelman's parents sent Rysio to stay with an aunt, with whom they believed he would be safe. The aunt poisoned herself along with Rysio and two other young family members in her care so that the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazis</a> would not take them to the <a href="/wiki/Extermination_camp" title="Extermination camp">extermination camps</a>. After the war, the Spiegelmans, unable to accept that Rysio was dead, searched orphanages all over Europe in the hope of finding him. Spiegelman talked of having a sort of <a href="/wiki/Sibling_rivalry" title="Sibling rivalry">sibling rivalry</a> with his "ghost brother"âhe felt unable to compete with an "ideal" brother who "never threw tantrums or got in any kind of trouble".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirsch201137_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirsch201137-6">[6]</a></sup> Of 85 Spiegelman relatives alive at the beginning of World WarÂ II, only 13 are known to have survived the Holocaust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKois2011_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKois2011-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_career">Life and career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Life and career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hs-art-design.jpg"><img alt="High School of Art and Design building" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hs-art-design.jpg/220px-Hs-art-design.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hs-art-design.jpg/330px-Hs-art-design.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hs-art-design.jpg/440px-Hs-art-design.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hs-art-design.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spiegelman graduated from the <a href="/wiki/High_School_of_Art_and_Design" title="High School of Art and Design">High School of Art and Design</a> in 1965.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spiegelman was born Itzhak Avraham ben Zeev<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpiegelman201118_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpiegelman201118-1">[1]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, Sweden, on February 15, 1948. He immigrated with his parents to the US in 1951.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxvii_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxvii-8">[8]</a></sup> Upon immigration his name was registered as Arthur Isadore, but he later had his given name changed to Art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpiegelman201118_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpiegelman201118-1">[1]</a></sup> Initially the family settled in <a href="/wiki/Norristown,_Pennsylvania" title="Norristown, Pennsylvania">Norristown, Pennsylvania</a>, and then relocated to <a href="/wiki/Rego_Park,_Queens" title="Rego Park, Queens">Rego Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">Queens</a>, New York City, in 1957. He began cartooning in 1960<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxvii_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxvii-8">[8]</a></sup> and imitated the style of his favorite <a href="/wiki/Comic_book" title="Comic book">comic books</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Mad_(magazine)" title="Mad (magazine)">Mad</a></i>. At Russell Sage Junior High School, where he was an <a href="/wiki/Honors_student" title="Honors student">honors student</a>, he produced the <i>Mad</i>-inspired <a href="/wiki/Fanzine" title="Fanzine">fanzine</a> <i>BlasÃ©</i>. He was earning money from his drawing by the time he reached high school and sold artwork to the original <i><a href="/wiki/Long_Island_Press" title="Long Island Press">Long Island Press</a></i> and other outlets. His talent was such that he caught the eyes of <a href="/wiki/United_Media" title="United Media">United Features Syndicate</a>, who offered him the chance to produce a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comic_strip_syndication" title="Comic strip syndication">syndicated comic strip</a>. Dedicated to the idea of art as expression, he turned down this commercial opportunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1997401_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1997401-9">[9]</a></sup> He attended the <a href="/wiki/High_School_of_Art_and_Design" title="High School of Art and Design">High School of Art and Design</a> in Manhattan beginning in 1963. He met <a href="/wiki/Woody_Gelman" title="Woody Gelman">Woody Gelman</a>, the art director of <a href="/wiki/Topps" title="Topps">Topps Chewing Gum Company</a>, who encouraged Spiegelman to apply to Topps after graduating high school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxvii_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxvii-8">[8]</a></sup> At 15 Spiegelman received payment for his work from a Rego Park newspaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200856_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200856-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>After he graduated in 1965, Spiegelman's parents urged him to pursue the financial security of a career such as dentistry, but he chose instead to enrol at <a href="/wiki/Binghamton_University" title="Binghamton University">Harpur College</a> to study art and philosophy. While there, he got a freelance art job at Topps, which provided him with an income for the next two decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1997D.27Arcy2011_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1997D.27Arcy2011-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Binginebriateasylum.jpg"><img alt="Binghamton State Mental Hospital" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="674" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Binginebriateasylum.jpg/220px-Binginebriateasylum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Binginebriateasylum.jpg/330px-Binginebriateasylum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Binginebriateasylum.jpg/440px-Binginebriateasylum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Binginebriateasylum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
After Spiegelman's release from <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Inebriate_Asylum" title="New York State Inebriate Asylum">Binghamton State Mental Hospital</a>, his mother committed suicide.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spiegelman attended Harpur College from 1965 until 1968, where he worked as staff cartoonist for the college newspaper and edited a college humor magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxvii.E2.80.93xviii_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxvii.E2.80.93xviii-12">[12]</a></sup> After a summer internship when he was 18, Topps hired him for Gelman's Product Development Department<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJamieson2010116_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJamieson2010116-13">[13]</a></sup> as a creative consultant making trading cards and related products in 1966, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Wacky_Packages" title="Wacky Packages">Wacky Packages</a></i> series of parodic trading cards begun in 1967.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiegelman began selling self-published <a href="/wiki/Underground_comix" title="Underground comix">underground comix</a> on street corners in 1966. He had cartoons published in underground publications such as the <i><a href="/wiki/East_Village_Other" title="East Village Other">East Village Other</a></i> and traveled to San Francisco for a few months in 1967, where the underground comix scene was just beginning to burgeon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late winter 1968 Spiegelman suffered a brief but intense <a href="/wiki/Mental_breakdown" title="Mental breakdown">nervous breakdown</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006102Campbell200856_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006102Campbell200856-15">[15]</a></sup> which cut his university studies short.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii-14">[14]</a></sup> He has said that at the time he was taking <a href="/wiki/Lysergic_acid_diethylamide" title="Lysergic acid diethylamide">LSD</a> with great frequency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006102Campbell200856_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006102Campbell200856-15">[15]</a></sup> He spent a month in <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Inebriate_Asylum" title="New York State Inebriate Asylum">Binghamton State Mental Hospital</a>, and shortly after he got out his mother committed <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a> following the death of her only surviving brother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFathers2007122Gordon2004Horowitz1997401_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFathers2007122Gordon2004Horowitz1997401-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Underground_comix_.281971.E2.80.931977.29">Underground comix (1971â1977)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Underground comix (1971â1977)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1971, after several visits, Spiegelman moved to <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii-14">[14]</a></sup> and became a part of the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">countercultural</a> underground comix movement that had been developing there. Some of the comix he produced during this period include <i>The Compleat Mr. Infinity</i> (1970), a ten-page booklet of explicit comic strips, and <i>The Viper Vicar of Vice, Villainy and Vickedness</i> (1972),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1997402_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1997402-17">[17]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Transgressive_art" title="Transgressive art">transgressive</a> work in the vein of fellow underground cartoonist <a href="/wiki/S._Clay_Wilson" title="S. Clay Wilson">S.Â Clay Wilson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006103_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006103-18">[18]</a></sup> Spiegelman's work also appeared in underground magazines such as <i><a href="/wiki/Gothic_Blimp_Works" title="Gothic Blimp Works">Gothic Blimp Works</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Bijou_Funnies" title="Bijou Funnies">Bijou Funnies</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Young_Lust_(comics)" title="Young Lust (comics)">Young Lust</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii-14">[14]</a></sup> <i>Real Pulp</i>, and <i>Bizarre Sex</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpel2007144_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpel2007144-19">[19]</a></sup> and were in a variety of styles and genres as Spiegelman sought his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Writer%27s_voice" title="Writer's voice">artistic voice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006103_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006103-18">[18]</a></sup> He also did a number of cartoons for <a href="/wiki/Pornographic_magazine" title="Pornographic magazine">men's magazines</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Cavalier_(magazine)" title="Cavalier (magazine)">Cavalier</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dude_(magazine)" title="The Dude (magazine)">The Dude</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Gent_(magazine)" title="Gent (magazine)">Gent</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1972, <a href="/wiki/Justin_Green_(cartoonist)" title="Justin Green (cartoonist)">Justin Green</a> asked Spiegelman to do a three-page strip for the first issue of <i>Funny Aminals</i>  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek1989103_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek1989103-20">[20]</a></sup> He wanted to do one about racism, and at first considered a story<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008140_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008140-21">[21]</a></sup> with African-Americans as mice and cats taking on the role of the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConan2011_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConan2011-22">[22]</a></sup> Instead, he turned to the Holocaust that his parents had survived. He titled the strip "Maus" and depicted the Jews as mice persecuted by <i>die Katzen</i>, which were Nazis as cats. The narrator related the story to a mouse named "<a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse" title="Mickey Mouse">Mickey</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek1989103_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek1989103-20">[20]</a></sup> With this story Spiegelman felt he had found his voice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200856_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200856-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Seeing Green's revealingly autobiographical <i><a href="/wiki/Binky_Brown_Meets_the_Holy_Virgin_Mary" title="Binky Brown Meets the Holy Virgin Mary">Binky Brown Meets the Holy Virgin Mary</a></i> while in-progress in 1971 inspired Spiegelman to produce "Prisoner on the Hell Planet", an expressionistic work that dealt with his mother's suicide; it appeared in 1972 in <i>Short Order Comix</i> <abbr title="Number">#</abbr>1,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek198998_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek198998-23">[23]</a></sup> which he edited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxviii-14">[14]</a></sup> Spiegelman's work thereafter went through a phase of increasing formal experimentation;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChute2012413_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChute2012413-24">[24]</a></sup> the <i>Apex Treasury of Underground Comics</i> in 1974 quotes him: "As an art form the comic strip is barely in its infancy. So am I. Maybe we'll grow up together."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The often-reprinted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHatfield2012138_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHatfield2012138-26">[26]</a></sup> "Ace Hole, Midget Detective" of 1974 was a <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">Cubist</a>-style <a href="/wiki/Nonlinear_narrative" title="Nonlinear narrative">nonlinear</a> parody of <a href="/wiki/Hardboiled" title="Hardboiled">hardboiled crime fiction</a> full of <a href="/wiki/Non_sequitur_(literary_device)" title="Non sequitur (literary device)">non sequiturs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHatfield2012138Chute2012413_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHatfield2012138Chute2012413-27">[27]</a></sup> "A Day at the Circuits" of 1975 is a recursive single-page strip about alcoholism and depression in which the reader follows the character through multiple never-ending pathways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuskin201068_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuskin201068-28">[28]</a></sup> "Nervous Rex: The Malpractice Suite" of 1976 is made up of cut-out panels from the soap-opera comic strip <i><a href="/wiki/Rex_Morgan,_M.D." title="Rex Morgan, M.D.">Rex Morgan, M.D.</a></i> refashioned in such a way as to defy coherence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChute2012413_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChute2012413-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1973 Spiegelman edited a <a href="/wiki/Pornography" title="Pornography">pornographic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychedelia" title="Psychedelia">psychedelic</a> book of quotations and dedicated it to his mother. Co-edited with Bob Schneider, it was called <i>Whole Grains: A Book of Quotations</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothberg2000214Witek2007bxviii_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothberg2000214Witek2007bxviii-29">[29]</a></sup> In 1974â1975, he taught a studio cartooning class at the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Art_University" title="Academy of Art University">San Francisco Academy of Art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1997402_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1997402-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the mid-1970s, the underground comix movement was encountering a slowdown. To give cartoonists a safe berth, Spiegelman co-edited the anthology <i><a href="/wiki/Arcade_(comics_magazine)" title="Arcade (comics magazine)">Arcade</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Bill_Griffith" title="Bill Griffith">Bill Griffith</a>, in 1975 and 1976. <i>Arcade</i> was printed by <a href="/wiki/Print_Mint" title="Print Mint">The Print Mint</a> and lasted seven issues, five of which had covers by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Crumb" title="Robert Crumb">Robert Crumb</a>. It stood out from similar publications by having an editorial plan, in which Spiegelman and Griffith attempt to show how comics connect to the broader realms of artistic and literary culture. Spiegelman's own work in <i>Arcade</i> tended to be short and concerned with formal experimentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrishakovaRyan201067.E2.80.9368_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrishakovaRyan201067.E2.80.9368-30">[30]</a></sup> <i>Arcade</i> also introduced art from ages past, as well as contemporary literary pieces by writers such as <a href="/wiki/William_S._Burroughs" title="William S. Burroughs">William S. Burroughs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bukowski" title="Charles Bukowski">Charles Bukowski</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuhle2004252_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuhle2004252-31">[31]</a></sup> In 1975, Spiegelman moved back to New York City,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxix_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxix-32">[32]</a></sup> which put most of the editorial work for <i>Arcade</i> on the shoulders of Griffith and his cartoonist wife, <a href="/wiki/Diane_Noomin" title="Diane Noomin">Diane Noomin</a>. This, combined with distribution problems and retailer indifference, led to the magazine's 1976 demise. For a time, Spiegelman swore he would never edit another magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006108_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006108-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly" title="FranÃ§oise Mouly">FranÃ§oise Mouly</a>, an architectural student on a hiatus from her studies at the <a href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_nationale_sup%C3%A9rieure_des_Beaux-Arts" title="Ãcole nationale supÃ©rieure des Beaux-Arts">Beaux-Arts</a> in Paris, arrived in New York in 1974. While looking for comics from which to practice reading English, she came across <i><a href="/wiki/Arcade_(comics_magazine)" title="Arcade (comics magazine)">Arcade</a></i>. Avant-garde filmmaker friend <a href="/wiki/Ken_Jacobs" title="Ken Jacobs">Ken Jacobs</a> introduced Mouly and Spiegelman, when Spiegelman was visiting, but they did not immediately develop a mutual interest. Spiegelman moved back to New York later in the year. Occasionally the two ran across each other. After she read "Prisoner on the Hell Planet" Mouly felt the urge to contact him. An eight-hour phone call led to a deepening of their relationship. Spiegelman followed her to France when she had to return to fulfill obligations in her architecture course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeer201326.E2.80.9330_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeer201326.E2.80.9330-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiegelman introduced Mouly to the world of comics and helped her find work as a <a href="/wiki/Colorist" title="Colorist">colorist</a> for <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">Marvel Comics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeller2004137_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeller2004137-35">[35]</a></sup> After returning to the US in 1977, Mouly ran into visa problems, which the couple solved by getting married.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeer201341_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeer201341-36">[36]</a></sup> The couple began to make yearly trips to Europe to explore the comics scene, and brought back European comics to show to their circle of friends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeer201347.E2.80.9348_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeer201347.E2.80.9348-37">[37]</a></sup> Mouly assisted in putting together the lavish, oversized collection of Spiegelman's experimental strips <i><a href="/wiki/Breakdowns_(comics)" title="Breakdowns (comics)">Breakdowns</a></i> in 1977.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeer201345.E2.80.9347_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeer201345.E2.80.9347-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Raw_and_Maus_.281978.E2.80.931991.29"><i>Raw</i> and <i>Maus</i> (1978â1991)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Raw and Maus (1978â1991)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Auschwitz_entrance.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1184" data-file-width="1632" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6f/Auschwitz_entrance.JPG/220px-Auschwitz_entrance.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6f/Auschwitz_entrance.JPG/330px-Auschwitz_entrance.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6f/Auschwitz_entrance.JPG/440px-Auschwitz_entrance.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Auschwitz_entrance.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spiegelman visited the <a href="/wiki/Auschwitz_concentration_camp" title="Auschwitz concentration camp">Auschwitz concentration camp</a> in 1979 as research for <i><a href="/wiki/Maus" title="Maus">Maus</a></i>; his parents had been imprisoned there.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Breakdowns</i> suffered poor distribution and sales, and 30% of the print run was unusable due to printing errors, an experience that motivated Mouly to gain control over the printing process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeer201345.E2.80.9347_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeer201345.E2.80.9347-38">[38]</a></sup> She took courses in <a href="/wiki/Offset_printing" title="Offset printing">offset printing</a> and bought a printing press for her loft,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeer201349_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeer201349-39">[39]</a></sup> on which she was to print parts of<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006111.E2.80.93112_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006111.E2.80.93112-40">[40]</a></sup> a new magazine she insisted on launching with Spiegelman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006109_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006109-41">[41]</a></sup> With Mouly as publisher, Spiegelman and Mouly co-edited <i><a href="/wiki/Raw_(magazine)" title="Raw (magazine)">Raw</a></i> starting in July 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReid2007225_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReid2007225-42">[42]</a></sup> The first issue was subtitled "The Graphix Magazine of Postponed Suicides".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006109_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006109-41">[41]</a></sup> While it included work from such established underground cartoonists as Crumb and Griffith,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006108_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006108-33">[33]</a></sup> <i>Raw</i> focused on publishing artists who were virtually unknown, avant-garde cartoonists such as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Burns_(cartoonist)" title="Charles Burns (cartoonist)">Charles Burns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lynda_Barry" title="Lynda Barry">Lynda Barry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Ware" title="Chris Ware">Chris Ware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ben_Katchor" title="Ben Katchor">Ben Katchor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gary_Panter" title="Gary Panter">Gary Panter</a>, and introduced English-speaking audiences to translations of foreign works by <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Antonio_Mu%C3%B1oz" title="JosÃ© Antonio MuÃ±oz">JosÃ© MuÃ±oz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A9ri_Samba" title="ChÃ©ri Samba">ChÃ©ri Samba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joost_Swarte" title="Joost Swarte">Joost Swarte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoshiharu_Tsuge" title="Yoshiharu Tsuge">Yoshiharu Tsuge</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChute2012413_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChute2012413-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Tardi" title="Jacques Tardi">Jacques Tardi</a>, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006109_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006109-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the intention of creating a book-length work based on his father's recollections of the Holocaust<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008171_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008171-43">[43]</a></sup> Spiegelman began to interview his father again in 1978<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFathers2007125_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFathers2007125-44">[44]</a></sup> and made a research visit in 1979 to the <a href="/wiki/Auschwitz_concentration_camp" title="Auschwitz concentration camp">Auschwitz concentration camp</a>, where his parents had been imprisoned by the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlau2008_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlau2008-45">[45]</a></sup> The book, <i>Maus</i>, appeared one chapter at a time as an insert in <i>Raw</i> beginning with the second issue in December 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006113_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006113-46">[46]</a></sup> Spiegelman's father did not live to see its completion; he died on 18 August 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxix_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxix-32">[32]</a></sup> Spiegelman learned in 1985 that <a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">Steven Spielberg</a> was producing an animated film about Jewish mice who escape persecution in Eastern Europe by fleeing to the United States. Spiegelman was sure the film, <i><a href="/wiki/An_American_Tail" title="An American Tail">An American Tail</a></i> (1986), was inspired by <i>Maus</i> and became eager to have his unfinished book come out before the movie to avoid comparisons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006118Kaplan2008172_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006118Kaplan2008172-47">[47]</a></sup> He struggled to find a publisher<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKois2011_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKois2011-7">[7]</a></sup> until in 1986, after the publication in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> of a rave review of the work-in-progress, <a href="/wiki/Pantheon_Books" title="Pantheon Books">Pantheon</a> agreed to release a collection of the first six chapters. The volume was titled <i>Maus: A Survivor's Tale</i> and subtitled <i>My Father Bleeds History</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008171Kaplan2006118_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008171Kaplan2006118-48">[48]</a></sup> The book found a large audience, in part because it was sold in bookstores rather than in <a href="/wiki/Direct_market" title="Direct market">direct-market</a> comic shops, which by the 1980s had become the dominant outlet for comic books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006115_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006115-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Will_Eisner2.jpg"><img alt="Photo of an elderly man" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="509" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Will_Eisner2.jpg/170px-Will_Eisner2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Will_Eisner2.jpg/255px-Will_Eisner2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Will_Eisner2.jpg/340px-Will_Eisner2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Will_Eisner2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spiegelman and <a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner" title="Will Eisner">Will Eisner</a>, <i>(pictured in 1982)</i>, taught at the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Visual_Arts" title="School of Visual Arts">School of Visual Arts</a> from 1978 to 1987.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spiegelman began teaching at the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Visual_Arts" title="School of Visual Arts">School of Visual Arts</a> in New York in 1978, and continued until 1987,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxix_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxix-32">[32]</a></sup> teaching alongside his heroes <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Kurtzman" title="Harvey Kurtzman">Harvey Kurtzman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner" title="Will Eisner">Will Eisner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006111_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006111-50">[50]</a></sup> Spiegelman had an essay published in <i><a href="/wiki/Print_(magazine)" title="Print (magazine)">Print</a></i> entitled "Commix: An Idiosyncratic Historical and Aesthetic Overview".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxx_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxx-51">[51]</a></sup> In 1990 Spiegelman he had an essay called "High Art Lowdown" published in <i><a href="/wiki/Artforum" title="Artforum">Artforum</a></i> critiquing the <i>High/Low</i> exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">Museum of Modern Art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxx_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxx-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the wake of the success of the <a href="/wiki/Cabbage_Patch_Kids" title="Cabbage Patch Kids">Cabbage Patch Kids</a> series of dolls, Spiegelman created the card series <i><a href="/wiki/Garbage_Pail_Kids" title="Garbage Pail Kids">Garbage Pail Kids</a></i> for Topps in 1985. Similar to the <i>Wacky Packages</i> series, the <a href="/wiki/Gross_out" title="Gross out">gross-out</a> factor of the cards was controversial with parent groups, and its popularity started a gross-out fad among children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBellomo2010154_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBellomo2010154-52">[52]</a></sup> Spiegelman called Topps his "<a href="/wiki/House_of_Medici" title="House of Medici">Medici</a>" for the autonomy and financial freedom working for the company had given him. The relationship was nevertheless strained over issues of credit and ownership of the original artwork. In 1989 Topps auctioned off pieces of art Spiegelman had created rather than returning them to him, and Spiegelman broke the relation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007a_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007a-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1991, <i>Raw</i> <abbr title="Volume">Vol.</abbr> 1, <abbr title="Number">No.</abbr>3 was published; it was to be the last issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxx_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxx-51">[51]</a></sup> The closing chapter of <i>Maus</i> appeared not in <i>Raw</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006113_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006113-46">[46]</a></sup> but in the second volume of the graphic novel, which appeared later that year with the subtitle <i>And Here My Troubles Began</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxx_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxx-51">[51]</a></sup> <i>Maus</i> attracted an unprecedented amount of critical attention for a work of comics, including an exhibition at New York's <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">Museum of Modern Art</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShandler2014338_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShandler2014338-54">[54]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize_Special_Citations_and_Awards" title="Pulitzer Prize Special Citations and Awards">special Pulitzer Prize</a> in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiss199854FischerFischer2002Pulitzer_Prizes_staff_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiss199854FischerFischer2002Pulitzer_Prizes_staff-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_New_Yorker_.281992.E2.80.942001.29"><i>The New Yorker</i> (1992â2001)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The New Yorker (1992â2001)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_New_Yorker_wordmark.png"><img alt="The New Yorker logo" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="42" data-file-width="355" height="26" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/The_New_Yorker_wordmark.png/220px-The_New_Yorker_wordmark.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/The_New_Yorker_wordmark.png/330px-The_New_Yorker_wordmark.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/The_New_Yorker_wordmark.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_New_Yorker_wordmark.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spiegelman and Mouly began working for <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> in the early 1990s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hired by <a href="/wiki/Tina_Brown" title="Tina Brown">Tina Brown</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200859_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200859-56">[56]</a></sup> as a contributing artist in 1992, Spiegelman worked for <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> for ten years. Spiegelman's first cover appeared on the February 15, 1993, Valentine's Day issue and showed a black <a href="/wiki/West_Indian" title="West Indian">West Indian</a> woman and a <a href="/wiki/Hasidic_Judaism" title="Hasidic Judaism">Hasidic</a> man kissing. The cover caused turmoil at <i>The New Yorker</i> offices. Spiegelman intended it to reference the <a href="/wiki/Crown_Heights_riot" title="Crown Heights riot">Crown Heights riot</a> of 1991 in which racial tensions led to the murder of a Jewish <a href="/wiki/Yeshiva" title="Yeshiva">yeshiva</a> student.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMendelsohn2003180Campbell200859Witek2007bxx_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMendelsohn2003180Campbell200859Witek2007bxx-57">[57]</a></sup> Spiegelman had twenty-one <i>New Yorker</i> covers published,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006119_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006119-58">[58]</a></sup> and submitted a number which were rejected for being too outrageous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFox2012_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFox2012-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within <i>The New Yorker</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s pages, Spiegelman contributed strips such as a collaboration titled "In the Dumps" with children's illustrator <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Sendak" title="Maurice Sendak">Maurice Sendak</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeiss2012Witek2007bxx.E2.80.93xxi_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeiss2012Witek2007bxx.E2.80.93xxi-61">[60]</a></sup> and an obituary to <a href="/wiki/Charles_M._Schulz" title="Charles M. Schulz">CharlesÂ M. Schulz</a> titled "Abstract Thought is a Warm Puppy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxii_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxii-62">[61]</a></sup> An essay he had published there on <a href="/wiki/Jack_Cole_(artist)" title="Jack Cole (artist)">Jack Cole</a>, the creator of <a href="/wiki/Plastic_Man" title="Plastic Man">Plastic Man</a>, called "Forms Stretched to their Limits" was to form the basis for a book in 2001 about Cole called <i>Jack Cole and Plastic Man: Forms Stretched to their Limits</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxii_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxii-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The same year, Voyager Company published a CD-ROM version of <i>Maus</i> with extensive supplementary material called <i>The Complete Maus</i>, and Spiegelman illustrated a 1923 poem by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Moncure_March" title="Joseph Moncure March">Joseph Moncure March</a> called <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wild_Party_(poem)" title="The Wild Party (poem)">The Wild Party</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxi_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxi-63">[62]</a></sup> Spiegelman contributed the essay "Getting in Touch With My Inner Racist" in the September 1, 1997 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Mother_Jones_(magazine)" title="Mother Jones (magazine)">Mother Jones</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxi_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxi-63">[62]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ted_Rall.jpg"><img alt="Photo of a man seated and wearing glasses" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1203" data-file-width="1165" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Ted_Rall.jpg/220px-Ted_Rall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Ted_Rall.jpg/330px-Ted_Rall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Ted_Rall.jpg/440px-Ted_Rall.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ted_Rall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Editorial cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Ted_Rall" title="Ted Rall">Ted Rall</a> begrudged Spiegelman's influence in New York cartooning circles.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spiegelman's influence and connections in New York cartooning circles drew the ire of political cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Ted_Rall" title="Ted Rall">Ted Rall</a> in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200858_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200858-64">[63]</a></sup> In an article titled "The King of Comix" in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Village_Voice" title="The Village Voice">The Village Voice</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnold2001_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnold2001-65">[64]</a></sup> Rall accused Spiegelman of the power to "make or break" a cartoonist's career in New York, while denigrating Spiegelman as "a guy with one great book in him".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200858_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200858-64">[63]</a></sup> Cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Danny_Hellman" title="Danny Hellman">Danny Hellman</a> responded by sending a forged email under Rall's name to thirty professionals; the prank escalated until Rall launched a defamation suit against Hellman for $1.5Â million. Hellman published a "Legal Action Comics" benefit book to cover his legal costs, to which Spiegelman contributed a back-cover cartoon in which he relieves himself on a Rall-shaped urinal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnold2001_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnold2001-65">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1997, Spiegelman had his first children's book published: <i>Open Me... I'm a Dog</i>, with a narrator who tries to convince its readers that it is a dog via pop-ups and an attached leash.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPublishers_Weekly_staff1995_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPublishers_Weekly_staff1995-66">[65]</a></sup> From 2000 to 2003 Spiegelman and Mouly edited three issues of the children's comics anthology <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Lit" title="Little Lit">Little Lit</a></i>, with contributions from <i>Raw</i> alumni and children's book authors and illustrators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxii.E2.80.93xxiii_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxii.E2.80.93xxiii-67">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-September_11_.282001.E2.80.93present.29">Post-September 11 (2001âpresent)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-September 11 (2001âpresent)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WTC_smoking_on_9-11.jpeg"><img alt="Smoke flowing from World Trade Center buildings after terrorist attacks" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/WTC_smoking_on_9-11.jpeg/220px-WTC_smoking_on_9-11.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/WTC_smoking_on_9-11.jpeg/330px-WTC_smoking_on_9-11.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/WTC_smoking_on_9-11.jpeg/440px-WTC_smoking_on_9-11.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WTC_smoking_on_9-11.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> provoked Spiegelman to create <i><a href="/wiki/In_the_Shadow_of_No_Towers" title="In the Shadow of No Towers">In the Shadow of No Towers</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spiegelman lived close to the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_site" title="World Trade Center site">World Trade Center site</a>, which was known as "Ground Zero" after the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> that destroyed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973-2001)" title="World Trade Center (1973-2001)">World Trade Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaskindOmer-Sherman2010xxi_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaskindOmer-Sherman2010xxi-68">[67]</a></sup> Immediately following the attacks Spiegelman and Mouly rushed to their daughter Nadja's school, where Spiegelman's anxiety served only to increase his daughter's apprehensiveness over the situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006119_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006119-58">[58]</a></sup> Spiegelman and Mouly created a cover for the September 24 issue of <i>The New Yorker</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEASME_staff2005_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEASME_staff2005-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> which at first glance appears to be totally black, but upon close examination it reveals the silhouettes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973-2001)" title="World Trade Center (1973-2001)">World Trade Center</a> towers in a slightly darker shade of black. Mouly positioned the silhouettes so that the North Tower's antenna breaks into the "W" of <i>The New Yorker</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s logo. The towers were printed in black on a slightly darker black field employing standard four-color printing inks with an overprinted clear varnish. In some situations, the ghost images only became visible when the magazine was tilted toward a light source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEASME_staff2005_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEASME_staff2005-69">[68]</a></sup> Spiegelman was critical of the Bush administration and the mass media over their handling of the September 11 attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003264_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003264-71">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiegelman did not renew his <i>New Yorker</i> contract after 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHays2011_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHays2011-72">[71]</a></sup> He later quipped that he regretted leaving when he did, as he could have left in protest when the magazine ran a pro-<a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">invasion of Iraq</a> piece later in the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200860_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200860-73">[72]</a></sup> Spiegelman said his parting from <i>The New Yorker</i> was part of his general disappointment with "the widespread conformism of the mass media in the <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">Bush</a> era".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003263_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003263-74">[73]</a></sup> He said he felt like he was in "internal exile"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003264_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003264-71">[70]</a></sup> following September 11 attacks as the US media had become "conservative and timid"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003264_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003264-71">[70]</a></sup> and did not welcome the provocative art that he felt the need to create.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003264_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003264-71">[70]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Spiegelman asserted he left not over political differences, as had been widely reported,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHays2011_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHays2011-72">[71]</a></sup> but because <i>The New Yorker</i> was not interested in doing serialized work,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHays2011_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHays2011-72">[71]</a></sup> which he wanted to do with his next project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200860_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200860-73">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiegelman responded to the September 11 attacks with <i><a href="/wiki/In_the_Shadow_of_No_Towers" title="In the Shadow of No Towers">In the Shadow of No Towers</a></i>, commissioned by German newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Die_Zeit" title="Die Zeit">Die Zeit</a></i>, where it appeared throughout 2003. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Jewish_Daily_Forward" title="The Jewish Daily Forward">The Jewish Daily Forward</a></i> was the only American periodical to serialize the feature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003264_71-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorriere_della_Sera_staff2003264-71">[70]</a></sup> The collected work appeared in September 2004 as an oversized<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[b]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Board_book" title="Board book">board book</a> of two-page spreads which had to be turned on end to read.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChute2012414_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChute2012414-75">[74]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Honor%C3%A9_Daumier_-_Gargantua.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1078" data-file-width="1400" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier_-_Gargantua.jpg/220px-Honor%C3%A9_Daumier_-_Gargantua.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier_-_Gargantua.jpg/330px-Honor%C3%A9_Daumier_-_Gargantua.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier_-_Gargantua.jpg/440px-Honor%C3%A9_Daumier_-_Gargantua.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Honor%C3%A9_Daumier_-_Gargantua.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Gargantua", a cartoon critical of King <a href="/wiki/Louis_Philippe_I" title="Louis Philippe I">Louis PhilippeÂ I</a>, led to the imprisonment of its author, <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier" title="HonorÃ© Daumier">HonorÃ© Daumier</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the June 2006 edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Magazine" title="Harper's Magazine">Harper's Magazine</a></i> Spiegelman had an article published on the <a href="/wiki/Jyllands-Posten_Muhammad_cartoons_controversy" title="Jyllands-Posten Muhammad cartoons controversy">Jyllands-Posten Muhammad cartoons controversy</a>; some interpretations of Islamic law prohibit the <a href="/wiki/Depictions_of_Muhammad" title="Depictions of Muhammad">depiction of Muhammad</a>. The Canadian chain of booksellers <a href="/wiki/Indigo_Books_and_Music" title="Indigo Books and Music">Indigo</a> refused to sell the issue. Called "Drawing Blood: Outrageous Cartoons and the Art of Outrage", the article surveyed the sometimes dire effect political cartooning has for its creators, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier" title="HonorÃ© Daumier">HonorÃ© Daumier</a>, who spent time in prison for his satirical work; to <a href="/wiki/George_Grosz" title="George Grosz">George Grosz</a>, who faced exile. To Indigo the article seemed to promote the continuance of racial caricature. An internal memo advised Indigo staff to tell people: "the decision was made based on the fact that the content about to be published has been known to ignite demonstrations around the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams2006_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams2006-77">[75]</a></sup> In response to the cartoons, Iranian president <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad" title="Mahmoud Ahmadinejad">Mahmoud Ahmadinejad</a> called for submissions of antisemitic cartoons. Spiegelman produced a cartoon of a line of prisoners being led to the gas chambers; one stops to look at the corpses around him and says, "Ha! Ha! Ha! Whatâs really hilarious is that none of this is actually happening!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrean2008_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrean2008-78">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>To promote literacy in young children, Mouly encouraged publishers to publish comics for children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeer2013115_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeer2013115-79">[77]</a></sup> Disappointed by publishers' lack of response, from 2008 she self-published a line of easy readers called <a href="/wiki/Toon_Books" title="Toon Books">Toon Books</a>, by artists such as Spiegelman, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9e_French" title="RenÃ©e French">RenÃ©e French</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rutu_Modan" title="Rutu Modan">Rutu Modan</a>, and promotes the books to teachers and librarians for their educational value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeer2013116_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeer2013116-80">[78]</a></sup> Spiegelman's <i>Jack and the Box</i> was one of the inaugural books in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPublishers_Weekly_staff2008_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPublishers_Weekly_staff2008-81">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2008 Spiegelman reissued <i>Breakdowns</i> in an expanded edition including "Portrait of the Artist as a YoungÂ %@&amp;*!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon20141_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon20141-82">[80]</a></sup> an autobiographical strip that had been serialized in the <i><a href="/wiki/Virginia_Quarterly_Review" title="Virginia Quarterly Review">Virginia Quarterly Review</a></i> from 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxiii_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxxiii-83">[81]</a></sup> A volume drawn from Spiegelman's sketchbooks, <i>Be A Nose</i>, appeared in 2009. In 2011 <i><a href="/wiki/MetaMaus" title="MetaMaus">MetaMaus</a></i> followedâa book-length analysis of <i>Maus</i> by Spiegelman and <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Chute" title="Hillary Chute">Hillary Chute</a> with a DVD-ROM update of the earlier CD-ROM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeater2011_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeater2011-84">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Library_of_America" title="Library of America">Library of America</a> commissioned Spiegelman to edit the two-volume <i>Lynd Ward: Six Novels in Woodcuts</i>, which appeared in 2010, collecting all of <a href="/wiki/Lynd_Ward" title="Lynd Ward">Ward's</a> <a href="/wiki/Wordless_novel" title="Wordless novel">wordless novels</a> with an introduction and annotations by Spiegelman. The project led to a touring show in 2014 about wordless novels called <i>Wordless!</i> with live music by saxophonist <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Johnston" title="Phillip Johnston">Phillip Johnston</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArtsy2014_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArtsy2014-85">[83]</a></sup> <i>Art Spiegelman's Co-Mix: A Retrospective</i> dÃ©buted at AngoulÃªme in 2012 and by the end of 2014 had traveled to Paris, Cologne, Vancouver, New York, and Toronto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon20141_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon20141-82">[80]</a></sup> A book complementing the showed titled <i>Co-Mix: A Retrospective of Comics, Graphics, and Scraps</i> appeared in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandle2013_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandle2013-86">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, after six writers refused to sit on a panel at the <a href="/wiki/PEN_American_Center" title="PEN American Center">PEN American Center</a> in protest of the planned "freedom of expression courage award" for the satirical French periodical <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie_Hebdo" title="Charlie Hebdo">Charlie Hebdo</a></i> following the <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Hebdo_shooting" title="Charlie Hebdo shooting">shooting at its headquarters</a> earlier in the year, Spiegelman agreed to be one of the replacement hosts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChow2015_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChow2015-87">[85]</a></sup> along with other names in comics such as writer <a href="/wiki/Neil_Gaiman" title="Neil Gaiman">Neil Gaiman</a>. Spiegelman retracted a cover he had submitted to a Gaiman-edited "saying the unsayable" issue of <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i> when the management declined to print strip of Spiegelman's. The strip, titled "Notes from a First Amendment Fundamentalist", depicts Muhammad, and Spiegelman believed the rejection was censorship, though the magazine asserted it never intended to run the cartoon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrayewski2015Heer2015_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrayewski2015Heer2015-88">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_life">Private life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Private life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1878" data-file-width="1423" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly.JPG/170px-Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly.JPG/255px-Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly.JPG/340px-Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spiegelman married <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly" title="FranÃ§oise Mouly">FranÃ§oise Mouly</a> in 1977 <i>(pictured in 2015)</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spiegelman married <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7oise_Mouly" title="FranÃ§oise Mouly">FranÃ§oise Mouly</a> on July 12, 1977,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyers2011_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyers2011-89">[87]</a></sup> in a City Hall ceremony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeer201341_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeer201341-36">[36]</a></sup> They remarried later in the year after Mouly <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Judaism" title="Conversion to Judaism">converted to Judaism</a> to please Spiegelman's father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeer201341_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeer201341-36">[36]</a></sup> Mouly and Spiegelman have two children together: a daughter <a href="/wiki/Nadja_Spiegelman" title="Nadja Spiegelman">Nadja Rachel</a>, born in 1987,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyers2011_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyers2011-89">[87]</a></sup> and a son Dashiell Alan, born in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyers2011_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyers2011-89">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Style">Style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"All comic-strip drawings must function as diagrams, simplified picture-words that indicate more than they show."</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>ââArt Spiegelman<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECates201096_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECates201096-90">[88]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Spiegelman suffers from a <a href="/wiki/Amblyopia" title="Amblyopia">lazy eye</a>, and thus lacks <a href="/wiki/Depth_perception" title="Depth perception">depth perception</a>. He says his art style is "really a result of [his] deficiencies". His is a style of labored simplicity, with dense visual motifs which often go unnoticed upon first viewing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200856.E2.80.9457_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200856.E2.80.9457-91">[89]</a></sup> He sees comics as "very condensed thought structures", more akin to poetry than prose, which need careful, time-consuming planning that their seeming simplicity belies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200861_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200861-92">[90]</a></sup>Spiegelman's work prominently displays his concern with form, and pushing the boundaries of what is and is not comics. Early in the underground comix era, Spiegelman proclaimed to Robert Crumb, "Time is an illusion that can be shattered in comics! Showing the same scene from different angles freezes it in time by turning the page into a diagramâan <a href="/wiki/Orthographic_projection" title="Orthographic projection">orthographic projection</a>!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChute2012412_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChute2012412-93">[91]</a></sup> His comics experiment with time, space, <a href="/wiki/Recursion" title="Recursion">recursion</a>, and representation. He uses the word "decode" to express the action of reading comics<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChute2012412.E2.80.93413_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChute2012412.E2.80.93413-94">[92]</a></sup> and sees comics as functioning best when expressed as diagrams, icons, or symbols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECates201096_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECates201096-90">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiegelman has stated he does not see himself primarily as a visual artist, one who instinctively sketches or doodles. He has said he approaches his work as a writer as he lacks confidence in his graphic skills. He subjects his dialogue and visuals to constant revisionâhe reworked some dialogue balloons in <i>Maus</i> up to forty times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200857_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200857-95">[93]</a></sup> A critic in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i> compared Spiegelman's dialogue writing to a young <a href="/wiki/Philip_Roth" title="Philip Roth">Philip Roth</a> in his ability "to make the Jewish speech of several generations sound fresh and convincing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200857_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200857-95">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiegelman makes use of both old- and new-fashioned tools in his work. He prefers at times to work on paper on a drafting table, while at others he draws directly onto his computer using a <a href="/wiki/Digital_pen" title="Digital pen">digital pen</a> and electronic drawing tablet, or mixes methods, employing scanners and printers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200861_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200861-92">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frans_Masereel_-_Passionate_Journey_-_two_pages.jpg"><img alt="Two panels from wordless novel.  On the left, a man carries a woman through the woods.  On the right, a man looks at a nude in a studio." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="800" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Frans_Masereel_-_Passionate_Journey_-_two_pages.jpg/220px-Frans_Masereel_-_Passionate_Journey_-_two_pages.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Frans_Masereel_-_Passionate_Journey_-_two_pages.jpg/330px-Frans_Masereel_-_Passionate_Journey_-_two_pages.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Frans_Masereel_-_Passionate_Journey_-_two_pages.jpg/440px-Frans_Masereel_-_Passionate_Journey_-_two_pages.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frans_Masereel_-_Passionate_Journey_-_two_pages.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wordless <a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">woodcut</a> novels like those by <a href="/wiki/Frans_Masereel" title="Frans Masereel">Frans Masereel</a> were an early influence.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Harvey Kurtzman has been Spiegelman's strongest influence as a cartoonist, editor, and promoter of new talent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZuk2013700_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZuk2013700-96">[94]</a></sup> Chief among his other early cartooning influences include Will Eisner,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrahm2004_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrahm2004-97">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Stanley_(cartoonist)" title="John Stanley (cartoonist)">John Stanley</a>'s version of <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Lulu" title="Little Lulu">Little Lulu</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Winsor_McCay" title="Winsor McCay">Winsor McCay</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Nemo" title="Little Nemo">Little Nemo</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/George_Herriman" title="George Herriman">George Herriman</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Krazy_Kat" title="Krazy Kat">Krazy Kat</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZuk2013700_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZuk2013700-96">[94]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Krigstein" title="Bernard Krigstein">Bernard Krigstein</a>'s short strip "<a href="/wiki/Impact_(EC_Comics)#Master_Race" title="Impact (EC Comics)">Master Race</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKannenberg200128_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKannenberg200128-98">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1960s Spiegelman read in comics <a href="/wiki/Fanzine" title="Fanzine">fanzines</a> about graphic artists such as <a href="/wiki/Frans_Masereel" title="Frans Masereel">Frans Masereel</a>, who had made <a href="/wiki/Wordless_novel" title="Wordless novel">wordless novels</a> in <a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">woodcut</a>. The discussions in those fanzines about making the <a href="/wiki/Great_American_Novel" title="Great American Novel">Great American Novel</a> in comics later acted as inspiration for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008171_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008171-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Justin_Green_(cartoonist)" title="Justin Green (cartoonist)">Justin Green</a>'s comic book <i><a href="/wiki/Binky_Brown_Meets_the_Holy_Virgin_Mary" title="Binky Brown Meets the Holy Virgin Mary">Binky Brown Meets the Holy Virgin Mary</a></i> (1972) motivated Spiegelman to open up and include autobiographical elements in his comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChute201018_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChute201018-99">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiegelman acknowledges <a href="/wiki/Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka">Franz Kafka</a> as an early influence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulman201086_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulman201086-100">[98]</a></sup> whom he says he has read since the age of 12,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKannenberg2007262_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKannenberg2007262-101">[99]</a></sup> and lists <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Nabokov" title="Vladimir Nabokov">Vladimir Nabokov</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Faulkner" title="William Faulkner">William Faulkner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gertrude_Stein" title="Gertrude Stein">Gertrude Stein</a> among the writers whose work "stayed with" him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1997404_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1997404-102">[100]</a></sup> He cites non-narrative avant-garde filmmakers from whom he has drawn heavily, including <a href="/wiki/Ken_Jacobs" title="Ken Jacobs">Ken Jacobs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stan_Brakhage" title="Stan Brakhage">Stan Brakhage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ernie_Gehr" title="Ernie Gehr">Ernie Gehr</a>, and other filmmakers such as <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a> and the makers of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Twilight_Zone" title="The Twilight Zone">The Twilight Zone</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZuk2013699.E2.80.93700_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZuk2013699.E2.80.93700-103">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs">Beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Spiegelman is a prominent advocate for the comics medium and comics literacy. He believes the medium echoes the way the human brain processes information. He has toured the US with a lecture called "Comix 101", examining its history and cultural importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006123_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006123-104">[102]</a></sup> He sees comics' low status in the late 20th century as having come down from where it was in the 1930s and 1940s, when comics "tended to appeal to an older audience of <a href="/wiki/G.I._(military)" title="G.I. (military)">GIs</a> and other adults".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200858.E2.80.9359_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200858.E2.80.9359-105">[103]</a></sup> Following the advent of the censorious <a href="/wiki/Comics_Code_Authority" title="Comics Code Authority">Comics Code Authority</a> in the mid-1950s, Spiegelman sees comics' potential as having stagnated until the rise of underground comix in the late 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200858.E2.80.9359_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200858.E2.80.9359-105">[103]</a></sup> He taught courses in the history and aesthetics of comics at schools such as the School of Visual Arts in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxix_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitek2007bxix-32">[32]</a></sup> As co-editor of <i>Raw</i>, he helped propel the careers of younger cartoonists whom he mentored, such as Chris Ware,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200860_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200860-73">[72]</a></sup> and published the work of his School of Visual Arts students, such as <a href="/wiki/Kazimieras_G._Prapuolenis" title="Kazimieras G. Prapuolenis">Kaz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drew_Friedman_(cartoonist)" title="Drew Friedman (cartoonist)">Drew Friedman</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Newgarden" title="Mark Newgarden">Mark Newgarden</a>. Some of the work published in <i>Raw</i> was originally turned in as class assignments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006111_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2006111-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiegelman has described himself politically as "firmly on the left side of the secular-fundamentalist divide" and a "<a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">1st Amendment</a> absolutist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrean2008_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrean2008-78">[76]</a></sup> As a supporter of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_speech" title="Free speech">free speech</a> Spiegelman is opposed to <a href="/wiki/Hate_speech" title="Hate speech">hate speech</a> laws. He wrote a critique in <i>Harper's</i> on the <a href="/wiki/Jyllands-Posten_Muhammad_cartoons_controversy" title="Jyllands-Posten Muhammad cartoons controversy">controversial Muhammad cartoons in the <i>Jyllands-Posten</i></a> in 2006; the issue was banned from <a href="/wiki/Indigo_Books_and_Music" title="Indigo Books and Music">Indigo</a>â<a href="/wiki/Chapters" title="Chapters">Chapters</a> stores in Canada. Spiegelman criticized American media for refusing to reprint the cartoons they reported on at the time of the <i>Charlie Hebdo</i> shooting in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrean2015_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrean2015-106">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiegelman is a non-practicing Jew and considers himself "a-Zionist"âneither pro- nor anti-<a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionist</a>; he has called <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> "a sad, failed idea".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHays2011_72-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHays2011-72">[71]</a></sup> He told Charles Schulz he was not religious, but identified with the "alienated <a href="/wiki/Jewish_diaspora" title="Jewish diaspora">diaspora</a> culture of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka">Kafka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Freud</a>Â ... what <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Stalin</a> pejoratively called rootless cosmopolitanism"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMendelsohn2003180_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMendelsohn2003180-107">[105]</a></sup>âa statement Ezra Mendelsohn interpreted as identification with "the Jewish spirit of universalism as championed by the greatest of Jewry's creative figures".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMendelsohn2003180_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMendelsohn2003180-107">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Spiegelman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Maus</i> looms large not only over Spiegelman's body of work, but over the comics medium itself.
